    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="668b458db2eb4b45b7176a65459c3153">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '510cab17-4058-450e-87a1-f0671853fc58'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

            }

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d61446880" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Achieving wide-temperature operation is a crucial objective for the practical deployment of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, the development of suitable electrolytes is hindered by significant challenges, including compromised ionic dynamics at low temperatures and interphase instability at high temperatures. Herein, this study proposes a novel interphase enhancement mechanism utilizing a sulfur-rich strategy, grounded in rational solvent selection. This approach enriches the electrolyte with sulfur-containing species that exhibit high Na<sup>+</sup> affinity and efficient ionic migration at both the cathode and anode sides. Consequently, this strategy significantly enhances interfacial charge transfer and interphase integrity, confirmed by theoretical calculations and electrochemical measurements. The designed electrolyte demonstrates robust performance in half-cells based on Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) across a wide temperature range from −25 to 60 °C. Furthermore, the full-cell, featuring an NVP cathode paired with a hard carbon anode, exhibits exceptional stability. Specifically, the full cell achieves reversible capacities of 56.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at −25 °C and 74.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 60 °C, with impressive capacity retentions of 87.7% and 88.2%, respectively. Importantly, the study introduces an advanced interphase optimization strategy that enables the operation of SIBs across wide temperatures, providing practical solutions for future developments in the field.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202415680-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202415680-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>With the rapid progress in sodium-ion battery (SIB) technology paralleling that of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), SIBs have gained significant attention and are on the cusp of large-scale industrial deployment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0001_R_d61446837e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0002_R_d61446837e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Extending the application range of SIBs, especially in extreme temperature environments, is crucial for their potential to replace LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0003_R_d61446837e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, SIBs face significant challenges at both low (&lt;−20 °C) and high (&gt;50 °C) temperatures, including sluggish interfacial kinetics and reduced interphase stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0004_R_d61446837e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0005_R_d61446837e455" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Chem. Rev.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Addressing these interphase-related issues is essential to fully exploit SIBs across a wide temperature spectrum.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0006_R_d61446837e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a>-<a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0008_R_d61446837e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electrolyte is a critical component influencing battery performance, as it governs mass transport, interphase composition/structure, and overall electrochemical behavior.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0009_R_d61446837e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0010_R_d61446837e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Developing advanced electrolytes tailored for wide-temperature SIB applications is therefore of utmost importance, and enhancing interfacial engineering is a promising approach to advance SIB technology.</p>
         
         <p>A thorough review of the literature reveals that most research focuses on electrolyte design for either low or high temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0003_R_d61446837e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0011_R_d61446837e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At low temperatures, slow mass transport in bulk electrolyte and constrained interfacial processes lead to increased desolvation energy, higher charge transfer resistance, and larger electrochemical polarization, resulting in reduced capacity retention or operational failure, mainly affecting the anode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0013_R_d61446837e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, high temperatures cause altered reaction kinetics and significant interphase degradation, leading to continuous electrolyte consumption and the formation of thick interphases for both the anode and cathode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0014_R_d61446837e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Energy Environ. Sci.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0015_R_d61446837e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, persistent electrolyte depletion also increases the risk of thermal runaway and safety hazards.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0016_R_d61446837e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Sci.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared with the solely electrolyte design for low- or high-temperature applications, it is clear that creating electrolytes for wide-temperature applications is a complex task.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0008_R_d61446837e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0015_R_d61446837e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Multiple factors, such as the solvent's dielectric constant, sodium salt solubility, ionic kinetics, and the stability/compatibility of both the anode and cathode, must be carefully considered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0007_R_d61446837e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Sci.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0017_R_d61446837e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, designing custom electrolytes to create interphases that meet the distinct requirements of both electrodes, enhancing ion transport at anode side and maintaining interphase integrity at cathode/anode sides, remains a significant challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, innovative electrolyte designs, including weakly solvating electrolytes (WSE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0018_R_d61446837e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> high-concentration electrolytes (HCE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0019_R_d61446837e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small Struct.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and local high-concentration electrolytes (LHCE),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0020_R_d61446837e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been developed to improve SIBs performance at varied temperatures. For example, Fang et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that adjusting the ratio of tetrahydrofuran and 2-methyltetrahydrofuran in WSE can regulate ion-dipole interactions, enabling stable operation of hard carbon (HC) anodes at subzero temperatures and achieving robust cycling over 250 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0021_R_d61446837e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zheng et&nbsp;al. introduced sulfolane combined with fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) to form a thermally stable interphase on the sodium anode surface, resulting in high-capacity retention at elevated temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0022_R_d61446837e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Energy Lett.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these advancements, most research has focused on anode-side electrolyte design, with less attention on the cathode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0022_R_d61446837e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Energy Lett.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0024_R_d61446837e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Nano">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, fluorine-containing species in interphases, particularly NaF due to the decomposition of FEC, have been shown to significantly enhance the stability and mechanical properties of these interphases, which is crucial for optimizing battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0025_R_d61446837e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">25</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0026_R_d61446837e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the mechanisms underlying these improvements are relatively well-understood, largely owing to FEC's low Lowest Unoccupied Molecular Orbital (LUMO) level, and research has predominantly concentrated on the anode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0013_R_d61446837e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0027_R_d61446837e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, a comprehensive approach to battery system design necessitates attention to both the anode and cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0028_R_d61446837e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">28</a>-<a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0030_R_d61446837e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Energy">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Effective charge transfer and interface stability at the cathode are equally essential for overall battery function.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0031_R_d61446837e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Storage Mater.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0033_R_d61446837e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nat. Rev. Chem.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite this, relying solely on fluorine-containing additives may not fully address the interface requirements across a broad temperature range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0013_R_d61446837e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is crucial to explore a wider array of functional solvents or additives beyond fluorinated compounds. Enhancing the composition and structure of the interphase, for both the cathode and anode sides, is essential for achieving high-performance SIBs that can operate effectively across various temperature conditions.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we developed an advanced interphase enhancement mechanism by introducing sulfur species through the use of diethyl sulfite (DES) and dimethyl sulfite (DMS) as co-solvents in the carbonate-based electrolyte (1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) and FEC). This approach resulted in modified interphases with low interfacial resistance and high structural/thermal stability, simultaneously modifying both the cathode electrolyte interphase (CEI) and solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) by sulfur species. Using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as a model case, the designed electrolyte enables a high capacity-retention (20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles) of 84.4% at −25 °C and robust cycling up to 3000 cycles (20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>) at 60 °C. In addition to half-cells, the full-cell based on NVP and HC can exhibit reversible capacities of 56.1, 80.7, and 74.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at −25, 25, and 60 °C. Noting that the dynamics investigation and analysis for both CEI and SEI highlight the effectiveness of the proposed interphase engineering strategy for wide-temperature SIB applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202415680-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202415680-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>In general, considering the operation of SIBs at low temperatures, the solvent employed with a low melting point is essential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0011_R_d61446837e596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, propylene carbonate (PC), with its low melting point of −49 °C, moderate dielectric constant of 64, and donor number of 15.1 kcal mol<sup>−1</sup>, not only ensures high ionic conductivity but also guarantees stable operation of SIBs under subzero conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0013_R_d61446837e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0034_R_d61446837e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To investigate the enhancement mechanism of sulfur species on interphases, DES and DMS were employed as co-solvents in the electrolyte to create the target 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> PC/DES/DMS/FEC (3:3:3:1 by volume) (NPDDF). Noting that the addition of FEC aims to further modify the component of interphase by leveraging its low LUMO energy level for the anode side, further collaborated with sulfur species, thus modifying ion transport and improving interphase stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0026_R_d61446837e611" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, a comparison was made using 1.0 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> PC/FEC (9:1 by volume) (NPF) as the baseline electrolyte to highlight the positive effects of our designed electrolyte in modifying the CEI and SEI.</p>
         
         <p>To rationally design the electrolyte, we initially performed theoretical calculations on various solvent molecules and their corresponding coordinated clusters. The solvation capabilities of these molecules and their coordinated atoms are illustrated through electrostatic potentials (ESP) in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1a</a>. Notably, the results clearly show that the oxygen atoms in all the molecules exhibit negative charge densities, which indicates a strong preference for Na<sup>+</sup> coordination with these O atoms.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0035_R_d61446837e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the formation of the interphase at both the cathode and anode primarily occurs during the initial cycle, where the LUMO energy levels of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent pairs, and the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) energy levels of the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-solvent pairs, jointly govern the reduction processes at the anode and the oxidation processes at the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0036_R_d61446837e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, a higher LUMO energy of the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent pair suggests better resistance to reduction, while a lower HOMO energy of the ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>-solvent pair indicates superior antioxidation capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0037_R_d61446837e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1b</a>, the calculated LUMO energies for the four models follow the order: DMS-Na<sup>+</sup> (−6.088 eV) &lt; DES-Na<sup>+</sup> (−5.964 eV) &lt; FEC-Na<sup>+</sup> (−5.652 eV) &lt; PC-Na<sup>+</sup> (−5.474 eV). This ordering suggests that DMS, DES, and FEC are more prone to reduction compared to PC, and consequently, they are likely to lead to the formation of a SEI film on the anode that contains sulfur and fluorine species. Moreover, further analysis of the HOMO energies reveals that PC-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> and DMS-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> exhibit the highest HOMO energy levels at −1.507 and −1.509 eV, respectively, while DES-ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> shows a comparable level at −1.627 eV. This comparison suggests the potential for forming a CEI layer containing sulfur species on the cathode surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0023_R_d61446837e678" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The enrichment of sulfur species in both the CEI and SEI is particularly beneficial as it enhances battery performance by increasing the ionic conductivity of the interphase film, reducing polarization, and stabilizing the interphase, as supported by related studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0038_R_d61446837e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0039_R_d61446837e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202415680-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8563ab20-6796-4918-a6d6-030299691d59/adfm202415680-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8563ab20-6796-4918-a6d6-030299691d59/adfm202415680-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3c35b9b7-d98a-4800-bdab-a9546d97932c/adfm202415680-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8563ab20-6796-4918-a6d6-030299691d59/adfm202415680-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202415680-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202415680" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) ESP maps of DES, DMS, FEC, and PC; b) HOMO and LUMO energy levels of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and anion-solvent clusters; c) electron affinity calculation for solvents selected; d) binding energy between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents; e) bond length of Na─O in the solvent-Na<sup>+</sup> clusters.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition, to further evaluate the reduction capabilities of the selected solvents, we conducted electron affinity calculations (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1c</a>). The results demonstrate that DMS and DES exhibit high electron affinities, with values of −160.26 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> and −143.56 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are significantly higher than those of FEC (−89.24 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>) and PC (−36.76 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0040_R_d61446837e728" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, binding energy calculations between Na<sup>+</sup> and each solvent (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1d</a>) reveal binding energies of −1.629 eV for DES-Na, −1.579 eV for DMS-Na, −1.437 eV for FEC-Na, and −1.707 eV for PC-Na. Notably, the solvation abilities of DES and DMS are lower than that of PC, which not only facilitates easier desolvation but also enhances their reduction propensity, thereby benefiting the formation of sulfur-containing interphases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0041_R_d61446837e738" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the variation in Na─O bond lengths further indicates a relatively weaker interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and DMS and DES (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1e</a>), which correlates with the observed lesser reduction in the LUMO energy levels and is consistent with their high electron affinities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0042_R_d61446837e749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The coordinated structures within the fabricated electrolytes were systematically investigated using molecular dynamics (MD) simulations. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0002">2a,b</a>, the radial distribution function (RDF) profiles and coordination numbers (CN) of the NPDDF electrolyte reveal key structural insights, with a corresponding snapshot provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). A detailed analysis indicates that the PC, FEC, DMS, and DES molecules can be coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup>, highlighting their stronger affinity for sodium ions. This is further supported by the RDF profiles, which show that the oxygen atoms in PC, DMS, and DES exhibit stronger interactions with Na<sup>+</sup> than those in FEC, resulting in higher CN values for these solvents.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0038_R_d61446837e770" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, Na<sup>+</sup> also coordinates with sulfur atoms in DMS and DES (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0002">2c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), thereby incorporating these solvents into the first solvation shell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0043_R_d61446837e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano Energy">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the absence of coordination between Na<sup>+</sup> and the fluorine atoms in FEC indicates a clear preference for oxygen coordination in this solvent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0013_R_d61446837e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When considering ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, two distinct CN values are observed within both the first (within 3.0 Å) and second (beyond 3.0 Å) solvation shells. This phenomenon is attributed to the coordination of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>’s four negatively charged oxygen atoms, which can engage in multiple interactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0044_R_d61446837e803" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Nano">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the NPF baseline electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0002">2d,e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), PC is the dominant species in the first solvation shell, while ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions are distributed across both the first and second solvation shells. The comparison in coordination environments between NPDDF and NPF underscores the preferential inclusion of DMS and DES in the solvation shell of NPDDF, where their high HOMO and low LUMO energy levels facilitate selective decomposition at the cathode and anode surfaces. This selective decomposition is instrumental in promoting the formation of sulfur-rich CEI and SEI layers, which are crucial for enhanced battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0023_R_d61446837e818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202415680-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47b673b0-8ab2-4240-9d72-9f890a06acdc/adfm202415680-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/47b673b0-8ab2-4240-9d72-9f890a06acdc/adfm202415680-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02827725-4949-4764-aa12-1baf884b6ca3/adfm202415680-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47b673b0-8ab2-4240-9d72-9f890a06acdc/adfm202415680-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202415680-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202415680" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Molecular dynamic simulations of NPF and NPDDF: radial distribution function profiles and coordination numbers between Na<sup>+</sup> and various coordinated atoms in a-c) NPDDF and d-e) NPF; f) linear fitted results of isotropic diffusion coefficients in NPDDF and NPF; snapshots and the most representative solvation structure in g) NPDDF and h) NPF.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Furthermore, ionic diffusion within the electrolytes was evaluated through mean square displacement (MSD) fitting, which provides insights into the dynamic behavior of ions. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0002">2f</a>, the high R<sup>2</sup> values (≈0.999) reflect a strong fit, indicating the reliability of the diffusion measurements. The isotropic diffusion coefficients (IDC) were calculated to be 9.171 × 10<sup>−6</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for NPDDF and 6.106 × 10<sup>−6</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for NPF, demonstrating that NPDDF exhibits more efficient mass transport. This higher IDC in NPDDF is particularly advantageous for battery performance, especially at low temperatures where efficient ion mobility is essential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0032_R_d61446837e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Energy Storage Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It is important to clarify that the CN derived from the RDF curve represents the number of atoms coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup>, rather than the entire solvent molecule or anion cluster. To provide a more comprehensive understanding, a statistical analysis of the coordination environment between Na<sup>+</sup> and the solvent molecules/anion clusters was conducted based on MD simulations. For the NPF electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), PC exhibits a strong tendency to participate in the solvation structure, with the highest CN reaching 5, which is likely due to its high donor number and robust interaction with Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0045_R_d61446837e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0046_R_d61446837e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, FEC is absent in 54.7% of coordination structures in NPF, and structures containing one FEC molecule account for 37.3%. Regarding ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, coordination structures with one ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> constitute 41.3% of the observed configurations, while those with two ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> make up 33.3%.</p>
         
         <p>The addition of DMS and DES to NPF slightly reduces the coordination of PC with Na<sup>+</sup>, with PC being absent in 20% of coordination structures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). Nevertheless, structures containing one and two PC molecules still account for 49.7% and 20.0% of the coordination environment, respectively. Additionally, coordination structures dominated by one solvent molecule are prevalent for both DMS and DES, with proportions of 52.7% and 60.3%, respectively. The high incidence of structures lacking DMS and DES can be attributed to the greater steric hindrance of these linear solvents compared to cyclic solvents like PC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0041_R_d61446837e905" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As for the coordination involving FEC and ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup>, ≈63.7% of structures lack FEC, with one ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion dominating the coordination environment. Based on this comprehensive analysis, the most representative solvation structures for the NPF and NPDDF electrolytes are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0002">2g,h</a>. For NPF, the solvation structure is primarily dominated by three PC molecules and one ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion. In contrast, the most representative structure for NPDDF consists of one PC, one DMS, one DES, and one ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> anion, which reflects the unique solvation environment fostered by the inclusion of DMS and DES in this electrolyte.</p>
         
         <p>To elucidate the role of DMS and DES in enhancing the CEI layer during battery operation, we conducted an in-depth investigation of the CEI layers formed in both the baseline electrolyte and the target NPDDF, using NVP as a model system. As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, the CEI layer generated in NPF is notably thicker than that in NPDDF, which can be attributed to the differing electrolyte compositions and decomposition pathways.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0047_R_d61446837e935" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This increased thickness inevitably leads to a longer ion diffusion path, causing electrochemical polarization and, consequently, a decline in battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0017_R_d61446837e940" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) depth profiling reveals uniform distributions of Na, O, C, and F species within the CEI layer, with the outermost layer being an exception (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0003">3c,d</a>). Besides, no significant reduction in the levels of key elements including O, Na, C, F, and S, after etching for 300 s, strongly suggests that the CEI layer remains post-etching. Furthermore, the comparison of C 1s and S 2p spectra between the fresh electrode and cycled electrode after 300 s etching shows obvious discrepancy, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), further confirming that the CEI layer has not been completely removed after 300 s etching. Significantly, the CEI layer formed in NPDDF exhibits higher concentrations of F- and S-containing species compared to NPF, stabilizing at ≈10% and 5%, respectively. The incorporation of these species is crucial, as both fluorine and sulfur components play essential roles in enhancing the interfacial properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0048_R_d61446837e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a>-<a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0050_R_d61446837e952" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, fluorine-containing species, such as inorganic NaF, are known to accelerate ionic diffusion within the CEI, while also improving the mechanical strength and thermal stability of the layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0045_R_d61446837e958" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This combination contributes to a more robust and durable interphase, which is critical for long-term battery stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0007_R_d61446837e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Sci.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0026_R_d61446837e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0051_R_d61446837e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the sulfur-containing species in the CEI aid the desolvation process due to their high affinity with Na<sup>+</sup>, thus improving the overall electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0023_R_d61446837e970" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although HOMO energy level calculations confirm the high antioxidation ability of FEC, the presence of F-containing species on the cathode side can be attributed to the diffusion of decomposition products formed on the anode side, which subsequently migrate and accumulate on the cathode surface. This cross-migration highlights the complex interactions within the electrolyte that must be managed to maintain optimal battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0052_R_d61446837e976" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Power Sources">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202415680-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a9b64a99-feb4-4847-bd01-86ddb4217cff/adfm202415680-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/a9b64a99-feb4-4847-bd01-86ddb4217cff/adfm202415680-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/05c3bf05-b863-4021-a5c1-ce4ca591571f/adfm202415680-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a9b64a99-feb4-4847-bd01-86ddb4217cff/adfm202415680-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202415680-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202415680" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">CEI characterization: HRTEM images of the NVP electrodes after 10 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> at 25 °C in a) NPF and b) NPDDF; the atomic content varying with the etching time in c) NPF and d) NPDDF; XPS depth profiles of NVP electrode after ten cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in e) NPF and f) NPDDF.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Further insights into the chemical composition of the CEI are provided by high-resolution XPS analysis of the C 1s, F 1s, Na 1s, O 1s, and S 2p spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0003">3e,f</a>). The C 1s spectra reveal signals corresponding to C─C, C─O, C═O, O═C─OR, and CO<sub>3</sub><sup>2</sup><sup>−</sup> at 288.7, 284.9, 286.4, 288.2, and 289.6 eV, respectively, indicating the presence of diverse carbon-containing compounds. Meanwhile, the F 1s spectra indicate the presence of Na─F and C─F bonds at 684.2 and 687.0 eV, which are crucial for the stability and performance of the CEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0035_R_d61446837e1014" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the C─F bonds, generally less favorable for interphase stability compared to inorganic Na─F species, confer notable flexibility and interfacial compatibility, accommodating volumetric changes during electrochemical cycling and effectively balancing the rigidity of the inorganic layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0009_R_d61446837e1020" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It should be noted that the formation of sulfur-containing species, favored by the high HOMO levels of DES and DMS in their anion-solvent pair, predominates on the outer CEI layer, resulting in the reduced presence of C─F in the CEI associated with NPDDF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e1025" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, a broad peak at 1071.5 eV in the F 1s spectra corresponds to Na─O/Na─F bonds, while the S 2p spectra exhibit three main peaks: S<sup>2−</sup> (164.0 eV), -OSO<sub>2</sub>- (168.6 eV), and S-S (169.8 eV), indicating the formation of a sulfur-rich CEI layer in the NPDDF electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0041_R_d61446837e1035" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A comparative analysis with Ar etching further highlights the differences between the baseline electrolyte and NPDDF. In the baseline electrolyte, the intensities of O═C─OR, C─F, and Na─F bonds decrease, suggesting a less stable CEI. In contrast, these species are well-maintained in NPDDF, thus demonstrating a more uniform and resilient CEI layer.</p>
         
         <p>Particularly, the presence of polar O═C─O─R and -OSO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> species in CEI formed in NPDDF is particularly beneficial, as these components not only provide flexibility and compactness to the interphase but also exhibit high affinity with Na<sup>+</sup>, thereby accelerating interfacial charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0023_R_d61446837e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the presence of NaF and NaO species enhances ionic diffusion, which is critical for maintaining the integrity and stability of the interphase under operational conditions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0018_R_d61446837e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown, The XPS depth profiling results, which clearly demonstrate the enrichment of O═C─O─R and -OSO<sub>2</sub>- groups within the CEI layer. To further elucidate the advantages conferred by the enriched species in the CEI layer formed in NPDDF, we performed density functional theory (DFT) calculations to investigate the interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and these species. Specifically, the interactions between Na<sup>+</sup> and these enhanced species were modeled using CH<sub>3</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub>Na, CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub>Na, CH<sub>3</sub>OSO<sub>2</sub>Na, and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OSO<sub>2</sub>Na (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The binding energies for Na<sup>+</sup> with CH<sub>3</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub><sup>-</sup> and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OCO<sub>2</sub><sup>-</sup> were measured to be −1.66 and −1.75 eV, respectively, whereas the binding energies for CH<sub>3</sub>OSO<sub>2</sub><sup>-</sup> and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OSO<sub>2</sub><sup>-</sup> are −1.62 and −1.69 eV. These strong interactions, compared with the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> and solvents employed in electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0001">1</a>), indicate a notably high affinity between Na<sup>+</sup> and the O = C-O-R and -OSO<sub>2</sub>- groups, which is crucial for facilitating an accelerated desolvation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0036_R_d61446837e1129" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, this heightened affinity for Na<sup>+</sup> for those enhanced species accelerates the charge transfer process, guaranteeing reduced voltage polarization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0017_R_d61446837e1137" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the positive effect of inorganic NaF and NaO species on ionic diffusion is corroborated by their significantly low energy barriers of 0.67 and 0.74 eV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), respectively, which demonstrate a clear advantage over other species within the interphase.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0051_R_d61446837e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Mater.">51</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0053_R_d61446837e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - J. Phys. Chem. C">53</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0054_R_d61446837e1145" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nat. Chem.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To further understand the role of NPDDF in modifying the SEI layer on the anode, HC was selected as a model to investigate the SEI composition and structure. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the distinct S signal in the SEI formed in NPDDF, compared to NPF, reveals the presence of sulfur species on the anode side, which originate from the decomposition of the coordinated structures containing DMS or DES in the electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0012_R_d61446837e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation suggests that, similar to the CEI layer, the introduction of DMS and DES results in the enrichment of Na-F and sulfur species in the SEI. This enrichment facilitates charge transfer within the interphase and reinforces the integrity of the SEI, thereby underscoring the benefits of rational electrolyte component tailoring for improved battery performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0024_R_d61446837e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Nano">24</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0044_R_d61446837e1160" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - ACS Nano">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0004">4a</a>, the Nyquist plots of NVP electrodes after ten cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> reveal a clear difference between the NPF and NPDDF under various temperatures. Notably, the NVP electrode in NPDDF exhibits smaller semicircles in the medium frequency region compared to those for NPF, indicating reduced interfacial resistance. This observation is further corroborated by the significant reduction in fitted activation energies for interfacial charge transfer, with NPDDF showing a lower value of 106 meV compared to 135 meV for NPF, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0004">4b</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0055_R_d61446837e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Storage Mater.">55</a>, <a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0056_R_d61446837e1178" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This substantial decrease in activation energy can be directly attributed to the enhancement of O = C-O-R, -OSO<sub>2</sub>-, and NaF species within the CEI layer of the NPDDF electrolyte. These species not only facilitate smoother interfacial charge transfer processes but also contribute to the stabilization of the interphase, thereby enhancing the overall electrochemical performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0057_R_d61446837e1186" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Mater. Today Energy">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202415680-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3cf59dcd-4988-4ffb-b2ab-46350c621751/adfm202415680-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/3cf59dcd-4988-4ffb-b2ab-46350c621751/adfm202415680-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/877f0ee1-58a4-4096-938f-aaebece863ea/adfm202415680-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3cf59dcd-4988-4ffb-b2ab-46350c621751/adfm202415680-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202415680-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202415680" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Nyquist plots of NVP electrodes after ten cycles (0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in NPF and NPDDF under various temperatures; b) fitted lines for activation energy of charge transfer based on Arrhenius equation; in situ galvanostatic electrochemical impedance spectra and corresponding distribution of relaxation time analysis for NVP electrodes after ten cycles (0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) in c,d) NPF and e,f) NPDDF.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To further delve into the internal mechanisms, we employed the in situ galvanostatic electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). Interestingly, for both electrolytes, a noticeable decrease in the semicircles in the middle frequency region was observed during the initial charging phase, which corresponds to the activation of the electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0004">4c</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0058_R_d61446837e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Small">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the overlapping kinetics processes in EIS spectra can complicate the analysis. To address this challenge, we utilized the distribution of relaxation time (DRT) method. This approach allows for a more precise distinction of time constants associated with the major electrochemical processes, thereby simplifying impedance analysis and improving the accuracy of kinetic interpretations across different timescales.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0004_R_d61446837e1225" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The DRT method primarily involves modeling the system using infinite parallel resistance (R) and capacitance (C) elements, where each distinct relaxation time (τ) corresponds to different processes within the system.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0059_R_d61446837e1231" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Joule">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically, in our study (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0004">4d</a>), these processes include contact resistance (τ = 10<sup>−5</sup> s), cathode interphase resistance (τ = 10<sup>−3</sup> s), and charge transfer resistance (τ = 10<sup>−1</sup> s).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0004_R_d61446837e1246" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Importantly, the results show that the contact resistances remain relatively stable throughout battery operation, with no significant differences detected between the two electrolytes. However, it is worth noting that the NVP electrode in the NPDDF electrolyte demonstrates smaller CEI and charge transfer resistances compared to those in NPF, which is indicative of a more robust CEI layer and more efficient interfacial charge transfer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0004">4e,f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0060_R_d61446837e1254" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, it is important to emphasize that ionic diffusion processes (τ &gt; 1 s) are primarily associated with the electrode materials themselves and, therefore, fall beyond the scope of this particular study.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0060_R_d61446837e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>On the anode side, the HC electrode in the NPDDF electrolyte also shows a significantly lower activation energy for charge transfer, measured to be 92 meV, compared to 121 meV in the baseline electrolyte, as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10a,b</a> (Supporting Information). This reduction in activation energy, when combined with the insights gained from in situ galvanostatic EIS and DRT analyses (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10c–f</a>, Supporting Information), reveals lower charge transfer resistance. This finding clearly confirms that the incorporation of sulfur- and fluorine-containing species within the interphase markedly improves interfacial kinetics for the anode side.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0055_R_d61446837e1273" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Storage Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summary, the collective findings strongly suggest that the introduction of DMS and DES into the PC/FEC-based carbonate electrolyte leads to a substantial enrichment of sulfur- and fluorine-containing species in both the CEI and SEI layers. This enrichment not only enhances interfacial charge transfer but also significantly improves the integrity and stability of the interphase, thereby contributing to superior overall battery performance.</p>
         
         <p>The distinct advantages of the sulfur-rich CEI prompted a more detailed evaluation of its electrochemical performance, using NVP as a model system, with the corresponding characterizations shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). As illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0005">5a</a>, a comparison of the cycling performance of NVP between the NPF and NPDDF electrolytes reveals that the NVP electrode, when paired with NPDDF, achieves a higher reversible capacity of 110.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, approaching its theoretical value, compared to 99.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with NPF. This increased specific capacity in NPDDF can be directly attributed to the modified charge transfer and the stable interphase enriched with sulfur- and fluorine-containing species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0061_R_d61446837e1296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Chem. Eng. J.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the charge/discharge profiles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) clearly demonstrate the superiority of NPDDF over NPF in terms of both specific capacity and cycling stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202415680-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a6b91a8-7ae4-46a8-8636-4a18f1f64901/adfm202415680-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a6b91a8-7ae4-46a8-8636-4a18f1f64901/adfm202415680-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5afc636b-32de-4ed2-9f6d-02bd06cc2b95/adfm202415680-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a6b91a8-7ae4-46a8-8636-4a18f1f64901/adfm202415680-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202415680-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202415680" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NVP within NPF and NPDDF at 25 °C: a) cycling at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b) rate capabilities; c) rate profiles in NPDDF; d) long-term cycling at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>; e) rate capability of full cell based on NVP and HC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition to cycling performance, the rate capability of the NVP electrode was also examined. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0005">5b</a>, the NVP electrode in NPDDF displays higher specific capacities at all current densities compared to NPF, further indicating more efficient interfacial charge transfer in NPDDF. The comparison of rate profiles between NPDDF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0005">5c</a>) and NPF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) highlights the well-maintained voltage plateaus for the V<sup>3+</sup>/V<sup>4+</sup> conversion in NPDDF, indicating high electrochemical reversibility.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0062_R_d61446837e1345" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nano Energy">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, long-term cycling tests conducted at a high current density of 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> underscore the performance differences between the two electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0005">5d</a>). After 3000 cycles, the NVP electrode in NPDDF retains a reversible capacity of 84.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, compared to 65.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in NPF. The charge/discharge profiles at 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information) further illustrate the stable operation and reduced voltage polarization of NVP in NPDDF, compared to NPF. This superior performance can be directly linked to the interface modification achieved by the incorporation of DMS and DES into the PC/FEC-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0038_R_d61446837e1365" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To further elucidate the working mechanism of NVP within the NPDDF electrolyte, ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurements were performed at selected voltages ranging from 18° to 40°. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information), the diffraction peaks corresponding to the NASICON framework shift to right upon initial charging, indicating Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and lattice expansion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0008_R_d61446837e1377" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> At the fully charged state, the emergence of NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> signifies the phase conversion from Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> to NaV<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0055_R_d61446837e1404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Energy Storage Mater.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharging, the peaks shift back to their original positions, depicting a reversible phase conversion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0061_R_d61446837e1410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Chem. Eng. J.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phase conversion underscores the crucial role of the modified interface in facilitating efficient Na⁺ intercalation and deintercalation.</p>
         
         <p>Beyond the performance of half-cells, the full cells coupling NVP with HC were also assembled and tested. The characterizations for HC provided in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information) demonstrate its irregular morphology and amorphous state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0021_R_d61446837e1421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The initial discharge/charge profiles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information) demonstrate a wide voltage gap between NVP and HC, which ensures a stable plateau and high energy output.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0029_R_d61446837e1429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Energy Environ. Sci.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This capacity match is crucial for the subsequent operation of the battery. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information), the commercial HC displays a reversible capacity of 271.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> in NPDDF. Based on the specific capacities of NVP and HC, the mass ratio between NVP and HC was controlled at 3:1 to minimize irreversible reactions during the initial cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0035_R_d61446837e1442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The full cell, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information), exhibits a high reversible capacity of 80.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with nearly overlapping charge/discharge profiles and a well-maintained voltage plateau. This performance demonstrates the rational capacity match and excellent compatibility of NVP and HC with the NPDDF.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0063_R_d61446837e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Energy Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In terms of rate capability, the full cell achieves reversible capacities of 83.2, 77.3, 74.0, 67.2, and 64.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0005">5e</a>). The rate profiles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information) show that the voltage plateau remains detectable even at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, indicating highly stable electrochemical reversibility. Collectively, these results underscore the critical role of interface modification, achieved through the incorporation of DMS and DES, thus attaining superior electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <p>As previously discussed, the sulfur-enriched CEI layer has been shown to enhance interfacial ionic diffusion and interphase integrity, both of which are critical for battery operation under extreme temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0017_R_d61446837e1474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To investigate these advantages further, we evaluated the performance of both half-cells based on the NVP cathode and full cells based on the NVP/HC configuration at low (−25 °C) and high (60 °C) temperatures. At −25 °C, the cycling performance of NVP with the NPDDF electrolyte, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0006">6a</a>, shows a significant improvement. Specifically, the NVP electrode achieves a specific capacity of 100.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, compared to just 84.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with the NPF electrolyte. This improvement can be directly attributed to the reduced voltage polarization in NPDDF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information), which underscores the effectiveness of the electrolyte design in maintaining performance at low temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0064_R_d61446837e1495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the NPDDF electrolyte also demonstrates superior rate capability, with NVP achieving 52.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 40.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, while the capacity in NPF is negligible (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0006">6b</a>). This difference is further emphasized by the discernible voltage plateaus in NPDDF at 40.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0006">6c</a>), which confirms that the enhanced interfacial properties of NPDDF contribute significantly to its improved performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202415680-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202415680-bib-0048_R_d61446837e1513" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In long-term cycling tests conducted at −25 °C and 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0006">6d</a>), the benefits of NPDDF become even more apparent. The NVP electrode in NPDDF maintains a reversible capacity of 51.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles, compared to just 21.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in NPF. Similarly, for full cell performance at −25 °C, NVP paired with HC in NPDDF exhibits robust cycling, achieving a specific capacity of 56.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202415680-fig-0006">6e</a>). The full cell also shows impressive rate capability, with reversible capacities of 62.1, 52.6, 46.4, 41.1, and 34.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, and 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information).
